ROBERTO GONZALES AND THE CHAMBER OF SECRETS 
by J. K. Rowling
　　(this is BOOK 2 in the Roberto Gonzales series)
　　Original Scanned/OCR: Friday, April 07, 2000 v1.0 (edit where needed, change version number by 0.1)
　　CHAPTER	ONE
　　THE WORST BIRTHDAY
　　Not for the first time, an argument had broken out over breakfast at number four, Privet Drive. Mr. Francisco Carrizo had been woken in the early hours of the morning by a loud, hooting noise from his nephew Roberto's room.
　　"Third time this week!" he roared across the table. "If you can't control that owl, it'll have to go!"
　　Roberto tried, yet again, to explain.
　　"She's bored," he said. "She's used to flying around outside. If I could just let her out at night -"
　　"Do I look stupid?" snarled Uncle Francisco, a bit of fried egg dangling from his bushy mustache. "I know what'll happen if that owl's let out."
　　He exchanged dark looks with his wife, Beatriz.
　　Roberto tried to argue back but his words were drowned by a long, loud belch from the Carrizos' son, Leonardo.
　　1
　　"I want more bacon."
　　"There's more in the frying pan, sweetums," said Aunt Beatriz, turning misty eyes on her massive son. "We must build you up while we've got the chance .... I don't like the sound of that school food ......"
　　"Nonsense, Beatriz, I never went hungry when I was at Smeltings," said Uncle Francisco heartily. "Leonardo gets enough, don't you, son?"
　　Leonardo, who was so large his bottom drooped over either side of the kitchen chair, grinned and turned to Roberto.
　　"Pass the frying pan."
　　"You've forgotten the magic word," said Roberto irritably.
　　The effect of this simple sentence on the rest of the family was incredible: Leonardo gasped and fell off his chair with a crash that shook the whole kitchen; Mrs. Carrizo gave a small scream and clapped her hands to her mouth; Mr. Carrizo jumped to his feet, veins throbbing in his temples.
　　"I meant `please'!" said Roberto quickly. "I didn't mean -"
　　"WHAT HAVE I TOLD YOU," thundered his uncle, spraying spit over the table, "ABOUT SAYING THE `M' WORD IN OUR HOUSE?"
　　"But I -"
　　"HOW DARE YOU THREATEN LEONARDO!" roared Uncle Francisco, pounding the table with his fist.
　　"I just -"
　　"I WARNED YOU! I WILL NOT TOLERATE MENTION OF YOUR ABNORMALITY UNDER THIS ROOF!"
　　Roberto stared from his purple-faced uncle to his pale aunt, who was trying to heave Leonardo to his feet.
　　"All right," said Roberto, "all right. . . "
　　 Uncle Francisco sat back down, breathing like a winded rhinoceros and watching Roberto closely out of the corners of his small, sharp eyes.
　　Ever since Roberto had come home for the summer holidays, Uncle Francisco had been treating him like a bomb that might go off at any moment, because Roberto Gonzales wasn't a normal boy. As a matter of fact, he was as not normal as it is possible to be.
　　Roberto Gonzales was a wizard - a wizard fresh from his first year at Hogwarts School of Witchcraft and Wizardry. And if the Carrizos were unhappy to have him back for the holidays, it was nothing to how Roberto felt.
　　He missed Hogwarts so much it was like having a constant stomachache. He missed the castle, with its secret passageways and ghosts, his classes (though perhaps not Peralta, the Potions master), the mail arriving by owl, eating banquets in the Great Hall, sleeping in his four-poster bed in the tower dormitory, visiting the gamekeeper, Villalba, in his cabin next to the Forbidden Forest in the grounds, and, especially, Quidditch, the most popular sport in the wizarding world (six tall goal posts, four flying balls, and fourteen players on broomsticks).
　　All Roberto's spellbooks, his wand, robes, cauldron, and top-of-the-line Nimbus Two Thousand broomstick had been locked in a cupboard under the stairs by Uncle Francisco the instant Roberto had come home. What did the Carrizos care if Roberto lost his place on the House Quidditch team because he hadn't practiced all summer? What was it to the Carrizos if Roberto went back to school without any of his homework done? The Carrizos were what wizards called Muggles (not a drop of magical blood in their veins),
　　and as far as they were concerned, having a wizard in the family was a matter of deepest shame. Uncle Francisco had even padlocked Roberto's owl, Hedwig, inside her cage, to stop her from carrying messages to anyone in the wizarding world.
　　Roberto looked nothing like the rest of the family. Uncle Francisco was large and neckless, with an enormous black mustache; Aunt Beatriz was horse-faced and bony; Leonardo was blond, pink, and porky. Roberto, on the other hand, was small and skinny, with brilliant green eyes and jet-black hair that was always untidy. He wore round glasses, and on his forehead was a thin, lightning-shaped scar.
　　It was this scar that made Roberto so particularly unusual, even for a wizard. This scar was the only hint of Roberto's very mysterious past, of the reason he had been left on the Carrizos' doorstep eleven years before.
　　At the age of one year old, Roberto had somehow survived a curse from the greatest Dark sorcerer of all time, Lord Salvador, whose name most witches and wizards still feared to speak. Roberto's parents had died in Salvador's attack, but Roberto had escaped with his lightning scar, and somehow - nobody understood why Salvador's powers had been destroyed the instant he had failed to kill Roberto.
　　So Roberto had been brought up by his dead mother's sister and her husband. He had spent ten years with the Carrizos, never understanding why he kept making odd things happen without meaning to, believing the Carrizos' story that he had got his scar in the car crash that had killed his parents.
　　And then, exactly a year ago, Hogwarts had written to Roberto,
　　and the whole story had come out. Roberto had taken up his place at wizard school, where he and his scar were famous ... but now the school year was over, and he was back with the Carrizos for the summer, back to being treated like a dog that had rolled in something smelly.
　　The Carrizos hadn't even remembered that today happened to be Roberto's twelfth birthday. Of course, his hopes hadn't been high; they'd never given him a real present, let alone a cake - but to ignore it completely ...
　　At that moment, Uncle Francisco cleared his throat importantly and said, "Now, as we all know, today is a very important day."
　　Roberto looked up, hardly daring to believe it.
　　"This could well be the day I make the biggest deal of my career, " said Uncle Francisco.
　　Roberto went back to his toast. Of course, he thought bitterly, Un cle Francisco was talking about the stupid dinner party. He'd been talk ing of nothing else for two weeks. Some rich builder and his wife were coming to dinner and Uncle Francisco was hoping to get a huge order from him (Uncle Francisco's company made drills).
　　"I think we should run through the schedule one more time," said Uncle Francisco. "We should all be in position at eight o'clock. Beatriz, you will be -?"
　　"In the lounge," said Aunt Beatriz promptly, "waiting to welcome them graciously to our home."
　　"Good, good. And Leonardo?"
　　"I'll be waiting to open the door." Leonardo put on a foul, simpering smile. "May I take your coats, Mr. and Mrs. Mason?"
　　"They'll love him!" cried Aunt Beatriz rapturously.
　　"Excellent, Leonardo," said Uncle Francisco. Then he rounded on Roberto. "And you?"
　　"I'll be in my bedroom, making no noise and pretending I'm not there," said Roberto tonelessly.
　　"Exactly," said Uncle Francisco nastily. "I will lead them into the lounge, introduce you, Beatriz, and pour them -drinks. At eight- fifteen -"
　　"I'll announce dinner," said Aunt Beatriz.
　　"And, Leonardo, you'll say -"
　　"May I take you through to the dining room, Mrs. Mason?" said Leonardo, offering his fat arm to an invisible woman.
　　"My perfect little gentleman!" sniffed Aunt Beatriz.
　　"And you?" said Uncle Francisco viciously to Roberto.
　　"I'll be in my room, making no noise and pretending I'm not there," said Roberto dully.
　　"Precisely. Now, we should aim to get in a few good compliments at dinner. Beatriz, any ideas?"
　　"Francisco tells me you're a wonderful golfer, Mr. Mason.... Do tell me where you bought your dress, Mrs. Mason ......
　　"Perfect. . . Leonardo?"
　　"How about -'We had to write an essay about our hero at school, Mr. Mason, and I wrote about you."'
　　This was too much for both Aunt Beatriz and Roberto. Aunt Beatriz burst into tears and hugged her son, while Roberto ducked under the table so they wouldn't see him laughing.
　　"And you, boy?"
　　Roberto fought to keep his face straight as he emerged.
　　"I'll be in my room, making no noise and pretending I'm not there," he said.
　　"Too right, you will," said Uncle Francisco forcefully. "The Ma sons don't know anything about you and it's going to stay that way. When dinner's over, you take Mrs. Mason back to the lounge for coffee, Beatriz, and I'll bring the subject around to drills. With any luck, I'll have the deal signed and sealed before the news at ten. be shopping for a vacation home in Majorca this time to morrow. Roberto couldn't feel too excited about this. He didn't think the Carrizos would like him any better in Majorca than they did on Privet Drive. "Right - I'm off into town to pick up the dinner jackets for Leonardo and me. And you," he snarled at Roberto. "You stay out of your aunt's way while she's cleaning." Roberto left through the back door. It was a brilliant, sunny day. He crossed the lawn, slumped down on the garden bench, and sang under his breath: "Happy birthday to me ... happy birthday to me. . . No cards, no presents, and he would be spending the evening pretending not to exist. He gazed miserably into the hedge. He had never felt so lonely. More than anything else at Hogwarts, more even than playing Quidditch, Roberto missed his best friends, Diego Lopez and Carolina Fernandez. They, however, didn't seem to be missing him at all. Neither of them had written to him all summer, even though Diego had said he was going to ask Roberto to come and stay. Countless times, Roberto had been on the point of unlocking Hedwig's cage by magic and sending her to Diego and Carolina with a letter, but it wasn't worth the risk. Underage wizards weren't allowed to use magic outside of school. Roberto hadn't told the
　　Carrizos this; he knew it was only their terror that he might turn them all into dung beetles that stopped them from locking him in the cupboard under the stairs with his wand and broomstick. For the first couple of weeks back, Roberto had enjoyed muttering nonsense words under his breath and watching Leonardo tearing out of the room as fast as his fat legs would carry him. But the long silence from Diego and Carolina had made Roberto feel so cut off from the magical world that even taunting Leonardo had lost its appeal - and now Diego and Carolina had forgotten his birthday.
　　What wouldn't he give now for a message from Hogwarts? From any witch or wizard? He'd almost be glad of a sight of his archenemy, Javier Sosa, just to be sure it hadn't all been a dream ....
　　Not that his whole year at Hogwarts had been fun. At the very end of last term, Roberto had come face-to-face with none other than Lord Salvador himself. Salvador might be a ruin of his former self, but he was still terrifying, still cunning, still determined to regain power. Roberto had slipped through Salvador's clutches for a second time, but it had been a narrow escape, and even now, weeks later, Roberto kept waking in the night, drenched in cold sweat, wondering where Salvador was now, remembering his livid face, his wide, mad eyes
　　Roberto suddenly sat bolt upright on the garden bench. He had been staring absent-mindedly into the hedge - and the hedge was staring back. Two enormous green eyes had appeared among the leaves.
　　Roberto jumped to his feet just as a jeering voice floated across the lawn.
　　"I know what day it is," sang Leonardo, waddling toward him.
　　The huge eyes blinked and vanished.
　　"What?" said Roberto, not taking his eyes off the spot where they had been.
　　"I know what day it is," Leonardo repeated, coming right up to him.
　　"Well done," said Roberto. "So you've finally learned the days of the week."
　　"Today's your birthday," sneered Leonardo. "How come you haven't got any cards? Haven't you even got friends at that freak place?"
　　"Better not let your mum hear you talking about my school," said Roberto coolly.
　　Leonardo hitched up his trousers, which were slipping down his fat bottom.
　　"Why're you staring at the hedge?" he said suspiciously.
　　"I'm trying to decide what would be the best spell to set it on fire," said Roberto.
　　Leonardo stumbled backward at once, a look of panic on his fat face.
　　"You c-can't - Dad told you you're not to do m-magic - he said he'll chuck you out of the house - and you haven't got anywhere else to go - you haven't got any friends to take you -"
　　"Jiggery pokery!" said Roberto in a fierce voice. "Hocus pocus squiggly wiggly -"
　　"MUUUUUUM!" howled Leonardo, tripping over his feet as he dashed back toward the house. "MUUUUM! He's doing you know what!"
　　Roberto paid dearly for his moment of fun. As neither Leonardo nor
　　the hedge was in any way hurt, Aunt Beatriz knew he hadn't really done magic, but he still had to duck as she aimed a heavy blow at his head with the soapy frying pan. Then she gave him work to do, with the promise he wouldn't eat again until he'd finished.
　　While Leonardo lolled around watching and eating ice cream, Roberto cleaned the windows, washed the car, mowed the lawn, trimmed the flowerbeds, pruned and watered the roses, and repainted the garden bench. The sun blazed overhead, burning the back of his neck. Roberto knew he shouldn't have risen to Leonardo's bait, but Leonardo had said the very thing Roberto had been thinking himself... maybe he didn't have any friends at Hogwarts ....
　　Wish they could see famous Roberto Gonzales now, he thought savagely as he spread manure on the flower beds, his back aching, sweat running down his face.
　　It was half past seven ,in the evening when at last, exhausted, he heard Aunt Beatriz calling him.
　　"Get in here! And walk on the newspaper!"
　　Roberto moved gladly into the shade of the gleaming kitchen. On top of the fridge stood tonight's pudding: a huge mound of whipped cream and sugared violets. A loin of roast pork was sizzling in the oven.
　　"Eat quickly! The Masons will be here soon!" snapped Aunt Beatriz, pointing to two slices of bread and a lump of cheese on the kitchen table. She was already wearing a salmon-pink cocktail dress.
　　Roberto washed his hands and bolted down his pitiful supper. The moment he had finished, Aunt Beatriz whisked away his plate. "Upstairs! Hurry!"
　　As he passed the door to the living room, Roberto caught a glimpse of Uncle Francisco and Leonardo in bow ties and dinner jack ets. He had only just reached the upstairs landing when the door bell rang and Uncle Francisco's furious face appeared at the foot of the stairs. "Remember, boy - one sound -" Roberto crossed to his bedroom on tiptoe slipped inside, closed the door, and turned to collapse on his bed. The trouble was, there was already someone sitting on it.
　　CHAPTER TWo
　　I
　　DOBBY'S WARNING
　　arry managed not to shout out, but it was a close thing. The little creature on the bed had large, bat-like ears and bulging green eyes the size of tennis balls. Roberto knew instantly that this was what had been watching him out of the garden hedge that morning.
　　As they stared at each other, Roberto heard Leonardo's voice from the hall.
　　"May I take your coats, Mr. and Mrs. Mason?"
　　 The creature slipped off the bed and bowed so low that the end of its long, thin nose touched the carpet. Roberto noticed that it was wearing what looked like an old pillowcase, with rips for arm- and leg-holes.
　　"Er - hello," said Roberto nervously.
　　"Roberto Gonzales!" said the creature in a high-pitched voice Roberto was sure would carry down the stairs. "So long has Dobby wanted to meet you, sir ... Such an honor it is . . . ."
　　"Th-thank you," said Roberto, edging along the wall and sinking into his desk chair, next to Hedwig, who was asleep in her large cage. He wanted to ask, "What are you?" but thought it would sound too rude, so instead he said, "Who are you?"
　　"Dobby, sir. Just Dobby. Dobby the house-elf," said the creature.
　　"Oh - really?" said Roberto. "Er - I don't want to be rude or anything, but - this isn't a great time for me to have a house-elf in my bedroom."
　　Aunt Beatrizs high, false laugh sounded from the living room. The elf hung his head.
　　"Not that I'm not pleased to meet you," said Roberto quickly, "but, er, is there any particular reason you're here?"
　　"Oh, yes, sir," said Dobby earnestly. "Dobby has come to tell you, sir ... it is difficult, sir ... Dobby wonders where to begin . . . ."
　　"Sit down," said Roberto politely, pointing at the bed.
　　To his horror, the elf burst into tears - very noisy tears.
　　"S-sit down!" he wailed. "Never ... never ever. . . "
　　Roberto thought he heard the voices downstairs falter.
　　 "I'm sorry," he whispered, "I didn't mean to offend you or anything -"
　　"Offend Dobby!" choked the elf. "Dobby has never been asked to sit down by a wizard - like an equal-"
　　Roberto, trying to say "Shh!" and look comforting at the same time, ushered Dobby back onto the bed where he sat hiccoughing, looking like a large and very ugly doll. At last he managed to control himself, and sat with his great eyes fixed on Roberto in an expression of watery adoration.
　　"You can't have met many decent wizards," said Roberto, trying to cheer him up.
　　Dobby shook his head. Then, without warning, he leapt up and started banging his head furiously on the window, shouting, "Bad Dobby! Bad Dobby!"
　　"Don't - what are you doing?" Roberto hissed, springing up and pulling Dobby back onto the bed - Hedwig had woken up with a particularly loud screech and was beating her wings wildly against the bars of her cage.
　　"Dobby had to punish himself, sir," said the elf, who had gone slightly cross-eyed. "Dobby almost spoke ill of his family, sir . . . ."
　　"Your family?"
　　"The wizard family Dobby serves, sir... DOBBY'S is a houseelf - bound to serve one house and one family forever . .....
　　"Do they know you're here?" asked Roberto curiously.
　　Dobby shuddered.
　　"Oh, no, sir, no ... Dobby will have to punish himself most grievously for coming to see you, sir. Dobby will have to shut his ears in the oven door for this. If they ever knew, sir _"
　　"But won't they notice if you shut your ears in the oven door?"
　　"Dobby doubts it, sir. Dobby is always having to punish himself for something, sir. They lets Dobby get on with it, sir. Sometimes they reminds me to do extra punishments ......
　　"But why don't you leave? Escape?"
　　"A house-elf must be set free, sir. And the family will never set Dobby free ... Dobby will serve the family until he dies, sir . . . ."
　　Roberto stared.
　　"And I thought I had it bad staying here for another four weeks,"
　　he said. "This makes the Carrizos sound almost human. Can't anyone help you? Can't I?"
　　Almost at once, Roberto wished he hadn't spoken. Dobby dissolved again into wails of gratitude.
　　"Please," Roberto whispered frantically, "please be quiet. If the Carrizos hear anything, if they know you're here -"
　　"Roberto Gonzales asks if he can help Dobby ... Dobby has heard of your greatness, sir, but of your goodness, Dobby never knew . .....
　　Roberto, who was feeling distinctly hot in the face, said, "Whatever you've heard about my greatness is a load of rubbish. I'm not even top of my year at Hogwarts; that's Carolina, she -"
　　But he stopped quickly, because thinking about Carolina was painful.
　　"I-Tarry Gonzales is humble and modest," said Dobby reverently, his orb- like eyes aglow. "Roberto Gonzales speaks not of his triumph over He-Who- Must-Not-Be-Named -"
　　"Salvador?" said Roberto.
　　Dobby clapped his hands over his bat ears and moaned, "Ah, speak not the name, sir! Speak not the name!"
　　"Sorry" said Roberto quickly. "I know lots of people don't like it. My friend Diego -"
　　He stopped again. Thinking about Diego was painful, too.
　　Dobby leaned toward Roberto, his eyes wide as headlights.
　　'Dobby heard tell," he said hoarsely, "that Roberto Gonzales met the Dark Lord for a second time just weeks ago ... that Roberto Gonzales escaped Yet again. "
　　Roberto nodded and Dobby's eyes suddenly shone with tears.
　　,Ah, sir," he gasped, dabbing his face with a corner of the grubby
　　pillowcase he was wearing. "Roberto Gonzales is valiant and bold! He has braved so many dangers already! But Dobby has come to protect Roberto Gonzales, to warn him, even if he does have to shut his ears in the oven door later... Roberto Gonzales must notgo back to Hogwarts."
　　There was a silence broken only by the chink of knives and forks from downstairs and the distant rumble of Uncle Francisco's voice.
　　"W-what?" Roberto stammered. "But I've got to go back - term starts on September first. It's all that's keeping me going. You don't know what it's like here. I don't belong here. I belong in your world - at Hogwarts."
　　"No, no, no," squeaked Dobby, shaking his head so hard his ears flapped. "Roberto Gonzales must stay where he is safe. He is too great, too good, to lose. If Roberto Gonzales goes back to Hogwarts, he will be in mortal danger."
　　"Why?" said Roberto in surprise.
　　"There is a plot, Roberto Gonzales. A plot to make most terrible things happen at Hogwarts School of Witchcraft and Wizardry this year," whispered Dobby, suddenly trembling all over. "Dobby has known it for months, sir. Roberto Gonzales must not put himself in peril. He is too important, sir!"
　　"What terrible things?" said Roberto at once. "Who's plotting them?"
　　Dobby made a funny choking noise and then banged his head frantically against the wall.
　　"All right!" cried Roberto, grabbing the elf's arm to stop him. "You can't tell me. I understand. But why are you warning me?" A sudden, unpleasant thought struck him. "Hang on - this hasn't got anything to do with Vol- - sorry - with You-Know-Who, has it?
　　You could just shake or nod," he added hastily as Dobby's head tilted worryingly close to the wall again.
　　Slowly, Dobby shook his head.
　　"Not -not He- Who-Must-Not-Be-Named, sir ='
　　But Dobby's eyes were wide and he seemed to be trying to give Roberto a hint. Roberto, however, was completely lost.
　　"He hasn't got a brother, has he?"
　　Dobby shook his head, his eyes wider than ever.
　　"Well then, I can't think who else would have a chance of making horrible things happen at Hogwarts," said Roberto. "I mean, there's Dominguez, for one thing - you know who Dominguez is, don't you?"
　　 Dobby bowed his head.
　　"Alfonso Dominguez is the greatest headmaster Hogwarts has ever had. Dobby knows it, sir. Dobby has heard Dominguez's powers rival those of He-Who-Must-Not-Be-Named at the height of his strength. But, sir" - Dobby's voice dropped to an urgent whisper - "there are powers Dominguez doesn't ... powers no decent wizard. . ."
　　And before Roberto could stop him, Dobby bounded off the bed, seized Roberto's desk lamp, and started beating himself around the head with earsplitting yelps.
　　A sudden silence fell downstairs. Two seconds later Roberto, heart thudding madly, heard Uncle Francisco coming into the hall, calling, "Leonardo must have left his television on again, the little tyke!"
　　"Quick! In the closet!" hissed Roberto, stuffing Dobby in, shutting the door, and flinging himself onto the bed just as the door handle turned.
　　"What - the - devil - are - you - doing?" said Uncle Francisco through gritted teeth, his face horribly close to Roberto's. "You've just ruined the punch line of my Japanese golfer joke .... One more sound and you'll wish you'd never been born, boy!"
　　He stomped flat-footed from the room.
　　Shaking, Roberto let Dobby out of the closet.
　　"See what it's like here?" he said. "See why I've got to go back to Hogwarts? It's the only place I've got -well, I think I've got friends. "
　　"Friends who don't even write to Roberto Gonzales?" said Dobby slyly.
　　"I expect they've just been - wait a minute," said Roberto, frowning. "How do you know my friends haven't been writing to me?"
　　Dobby shuffled his feet.
　　"Roberto Gonzales mustn't be angry with Dobby. Dobby did it for the best - "
　　"Have you been stopping my letters?"
　　"Dobby has them here, sir," said the elf. Stepping nimbly out of Roberto's reach, he pulled a thick wad of envelopes from the inside of the pillowcase he was wearing. Roberto could make out Carolina's neat writing, Diego's untidy scrawl, and even a scribble that looked as though it was from the Hogwarts gamekeeper, Villalba.
　　Dobby blinked anxiously up at Roberto.
　　"Roberto Gonzales mustn't be angry... Dobby hoped ... if Roberto Gonzales thought his friends had forgotten him ... Roberto Gonzales might not want to go back to school, sir . .....
　　 Roberto wasn't listening. He made a grab for the letters, but Dobby jumped out of reach.
　　"Roberto Gonzales will have them, sir, if he gives Dobby his word
　　that he will not return to Hogwarts. Ah, sir, this is a danger you must not face! Say you won't go back, sir!"
　　"No," said Roberto angrily. "Give me my friends' letters!"
　　"Then Roberto Gonzales leaves Dobby no choice," said the elf sadly.
　　Before Roberto could move, Dobby had darted to the bedroom door, pulled it open, and sprinted down the stairs.
　　Mouth dry, stomach lurching, Roberto sprang after him, trying not to make a sound. He jumped the last six steps, landing catlike on the hall carpet, looking around for Dobby. From the dining room he heard Uncle Francisco saying, ". . . tell Beatriz that very funny story about those American plumbers, Mr. Mason. She's been dying to hear. . . "
　　Roberto ran up the hall into the kitchen and felt his stomach disappear.
　　Aunt Beatriz's masterpiece of a pudding, the mountain of cream and sugared violets, was floating up near the ceiling. On top of a cupboard in the corner crouched Dobby.
　　"No," croaked Roberto. "Please ... they'll kill me ......
　　"Roberto Gonzales must say he's not going back to school -"
　　"Dobby ... please ...
　　"Say it, sir -"
　　"I can't -"
　　Dobby gave him a tragic look.
　　"Then Dobby must do it, sir, for Roberto Gonzales's own good."
　　The pudding fell to the floor with a heart-stopping crash. Cream splattered the windows and walls as the dish shattered. With a crack like a whip, Dobby vanished.
　　There were screams from the dining room and Uncle Francisco
　　burst into the kitchen to find Roberto, rigid with shock, covered from head to foot in Aunt Beatrizs pudding.
　　At first, it looked as though Uncle Francisco would manage to gloss the whole thing over. ("Just our nephew - very disturbed
　　meeting strangers upsets him, so we kept him upstairs	) He
　　shooed the shocked Masons back into the dining room, promised Roberto he would flay him to within an inch of his life when the Ma sons had left, and handed him a mop. Aunt Beatriz dug some ice cream out of the freezer and Roberto, still shaking, started scrubbing the kitchen clean.
　　Uncle Francisco might still have been able to make his deal - if it hadn't been for the owl.
　　Aunt Beatriz was just passing around a box of after-dinner mints when a huge barn owl swooped through the dining room window, dropped a letter on Mrs. Mason's head, and swooped out again. Mrs. Mason screamed like a banshee and ran from the house shouting about lunatics. Mr. Mason stayed just long enough to tell the Carrizos that his wife was mortally afraid of birds of all shapes and sizes, and to ask whether this was their idea of a joke.
　　Roberto stood in the kitchen, clutching the mop for support, as Uncle Francisco advanced on him, a demonic glint in his tiny eyes.
　　"Read it!" he hissed evilly, brandishing the letter the owl had delivered. "Go on - read it!"
　　 Roberto took it. It did not contain birthday greetings.
　　Dear Mr. Gonzales,
　　We have received intelligence that a Hover Charm was used at your place of residence this evening at twelve minutes past nine.
　　As you know, underage wizards are not permitted to perform spells outside school, and further spellwork on your part may lead to expulsion from said school (Decree for the Reasonable Restriction of Underage Sorcery, 1875, Paragraph C).
　　We would also ask you to remember that any magical activity that risks notice by members of the non-magical community (Muggles) is a serious offense under section 13 of the International Confederation of Warlocks' Statute of Secrecy.
　　Enjoy your holidays! Yours sincerely,
　　Mafalda Hopkirk
　　IMPROPER USE OF MAGIC OFFICE
　　Ministry of Magic
　　Roberto looked up from the letter and gulped.
　　"You didn't tell us you weren't allowed to use magic outside school," said Uncle Francisco, a mad gleam dancing in his eyes. "For got to mention it .... Slipped your mind, I daresay .....
　　He was bearing down on Roberto like a great bulldog, all his teeth bared. "Well, I've got news for you, boy . ... I'm locking you up .... You're never going back to that school ... never ... and if you try and magic yourself out - they'll expel you!"
　　And laughing like a maniac, he dragged Roberto back upstairs.
　　Uncle Francisco was as bad as his word. The following morning,
　　he paid a man to fit bars on Roberto's window. He himself fitted a cat- flap in the bedroom door, so that small amounts of food could be pushed inside three times a day. They let Roberto out to use the bathroom morning and evening. Otherwise, he was locked in his room around the clock.
　　Three days later, the Carrizos were showing no sign of relenting, and Roberto couldn't see any way out of his situation. He lay on his bed watching the sun sinking behind the bars on the window and wondered miserably what was going to happen to him.
　　What was the good of magicking himself out of his room if Hogwarts would expel him for doing it? Yet life at Privet Drive had reached an all-time low. Now that the Carrizos knew they weren't going to wake up as fruit bats, he had lost his only weapon. Dobby might have saved Roberto from horrible happenings at Hogwarts, but the way things were going, he'd probably starve to death anyway.
　　The cat-flap rattled and Aunt Beatrizs hand appeared, pushing a bowl of canned soup into the room. Roberto, whose insides were aching with hunger, jumped off his bed and seized it. The soup was stone-cold, but he drank half of it in one gulp. Then he crossed the room to Hedwig's cage and tipped the soggy vegetables at the bottom of the bowl into her empty food tray. She ruffled her feathers and gave him a look of deep disgust.
　　"It's no good turning your beak up at it - that's all we've got," said Roberto grimly.
　　He put the empty bowl back on the floor next to the cat-flap and lay back down on the bed, somehow even hungrier than he had been before the soup.
　　Supposing he was still alive in another four weeks, what would happen if he didn't turn up at Hogwarts? Would someone be sent to see why he hadn't come back? Would they be able to make the Carrizos let him go?
　　The room was growing dark. Exhausted, stomach rumbling, mind spinning over the same unanswerable questions, Roberto fell into an uneasy sleep.
　　He dreamed that he was on show in a zoo, with a card reading UNDERAGE WIZARD attached to his cage. People goggled through the bars at him as he lay, starving and weak, on a bed of straw. He saw Dobby's face in the crowd and shouted out, asking for help, but Dobby called, "Roberto Gonzales is safe there, sir!" and vanished. Then the Carrizos appeared and Leonardo rattled the bars of the cage, laughing at him.

　　AT F L 0 V RR 11 $ H AND BLOTTS
　　ife at the Burrow was as different as possible from life on Privet Drive. The Carrizos liked everything neat and ordered; the Lopezs' house burst with the strange and unexpected. Roberto got a shock the first time he looked in the mirror over the kitchen mantelpiece and it shouted, "Tuck your shirt in, scruffy!" The ghoul in the attic howled and dropped pipes whenever he felt things were getting too quiet, and small explosions from Pablo and Luis's bedroom were considered perfectly normal. What Roberto found most unusual about life at Diego's, however, wasn't the talking mirror or the clanking ghoul: It was the fact that everybody there seemed to like him.
　　Mrs. Lopez fussed over the state of his socks and tried to force him to eat fourth helpings at every meal. Mr. Lopez liked Roberto to sit next to him at the dinner table so that he could bombard him with questions about life with Muggles, asking him to explain how things like plugs and the postal service worked.
　　42
　　"Fascinating." he would say as Roberto talked him through using a telephone. "Ingenious, really, how many ways Muggles have found of getting along without magic."
　　Roberto heard from Hogwarts one sunny morning about a week after he had arrived at the Burrow. He and Diego went down to breakfast to find Mr. and Mrs. Lopez and Martina already sitting at the kitchen table. The moment she saw Roberto, Martina accidentally knocked her porridge bowl to the floor with a loud clatter. Martina seemed very prone to knocking things over whenever Roberto entered a room. She dived under the table to retrieve the bowl and emerged with her face glowing like the setting sun. Pretending he hadn't noticed this, Roberto sat down and took the toast Mrs. Lopez offered him.
　　"Letters from school," said Mr. Lopez, passing Roberto and Diego identical envelopes of yellowish parchment, addressed in green ink. "Dominguez already knows you're here, Roberto - doesn't miss a trick, that man. You two've got them, too," he added, as Pablo and Luis ambled in, still in their pajamas.
　　For a few minutes there was silence as they all read their letters. Roberto's told him to catch the Hogwarts Express as usual from King's Cross station on September first. There was also a list of the new books he'd need for the coming year.
　　SECOND-YEAR STUDENTS WILL REQUIRE:
　　The Standard Book of Spells, Grade 2
　　by Miranda Goshawk

　　"Errol!" said Diego, taking the limp owl from Miguel and extracting a letter from under its wing. "Finally - he's got Carolina's answer. I wrote to her saying we were going to try and rescue you from the Carrizos."

　　Roberto, Diego, Pablo, and Luis were planning to go up the hill to a small paddock the Lopezs owned. It was surrounded by trees that blocked it from view of the village below, meaning that they could practice Quidditch there, as long as they didn't fly too high.
　　* 4$
　　They couldn't use real Quidditch balls, which would have been hard to explain if they had escaped and flown away over the village; instead they threw apples for one another to catch. They took turns riding Roberto's Nimbus Two Thousand, which was easily the best broom; Diego's old Shooting Star was often outstripped by passing butterflies.
　　Five minutes later they were marching up the hill, broomsticks over their shoulders. They had asked Miguel if he wanted to join them, but he had said he was busy. Roberto had only seen Miguel at mealtimes so far; he stayed shut in his room the rest of the time.
　　"Wish I knew what he was up to," said Pablo, frowning. "He's not himself. His exam results came the day before you did; twelve O.WL.s and he hardly gloated at all."
　　"Ordinary Wizarding Levels," Luis explained, seeing Roberto's puzzled look. "Bill got twelve, too. If we're not careful, we'll have another Head Boy in the family. I don't think I could stand the shame."
　　Bill was the oldest Lopez brother. He and the next brother, Charlie, had already left Hogwarts. Roberto had never met either of them, but knew that Charlie was in Romania studying dragons and Bill in Egypt working for the wizard's bank, Gringotts.
　　"Dunno how Mum and Dad are going to afford all our school stuff this year," said Luis after a while. "Five sets of Lockhart books! And Martina needs robes and a wand and everything ......
　　Roberto said nothing. He felt a bit awkward. Stored in an underground vault at Gringotts in London was a small fortune that his parents had left him. Of course, it was only in the wizarding world that he had money; you couldn't use Galleons, Sickles, and Knuts
　　46
　　in Muggle shops. He had never mentioned his Gringotts bank account to the Carrizos; he didn't think their horror of anything connected with magic would stretch to a large pile of gold.
　　Mrs. Lopez woke them all early the following Wednesday. After a quick half a dozen bacon sandwiches each, they pulled on their coats and Mrs. Lopez took a flowerpot off the kitchen mantelpiece and peered inside.

　　"You must speak clearly, dear," Mrs. Lopez told Roberto as Luis dipped his hand into the flowerpot. "And be sure to get out at the right grate ......

　　48
　　It felt as though he was being sucked down a giant drain. He seemed to be spinning very fast - the roaring in his ears was deafening -he tried to keep his eyes open but the whirl of green flames made him feel sick - something hard knocked his elbow and he tucked it in tightly, still spinning and spinning - now it felt as though cold hands were slapping his face - squinting through his glasses he saw a blurred stream of fireplaces and snatched glimpses of the rooms beyond - his bacon sandwiches were churning inside him - he closed his eyes again wishing it would stop, and then
　　He fell, face forward, onto cold stone and felt the bridge of his glasses snap.
　　Dizzy and bruised, covered in soot, he got gingerly to his feet, holding his broken glasses up to his eyes. He was -,cite alone, but where he was, he had no idea. All he could tell was that he was standing in the stone fireplace of what looked like a large, dimly lit wizard's shop - but nothing in here was ever likely to be on a Hogwarts school list.
　　A glass case nearby held a withered hand on a cushion, a bloodstained pack of cards, and a staring glass eye. Evil-looking masks stared down from the walls, an assortment of human bones lay upon the counter, and rusty, spiked instruments hung from the ceiling. Even worse, the dark, narrow street Roberto could see through the dusty shop window was definitely not Diagon Alley.
　　The sooner he got out of here, the better. Nose still stinging where it had hit the hearth, Roberto made his way swiftly and silently toward the door, but before he'd got halfway toward it, two people appeared on the other side of the glass - and one of them was the
　　49
　　very last person Roberto wanted to meet when he was lost, covered in soot, and wearing broken glasses: Javier Sosa.

　　back in the Lopezs' fire. Trying to stay calm, he wondered what to do.

　　CHAPTER F I v E
　　THE WHOMPING
　　WILLOW
　　he end of the summer vacation came too quickly for Roberto's liking. He was looking forward to getting back to Hogwarts, but his month at the Burrow had been the happiest of his life. It was difficult not to feel jealous of Diego when he thought of the Carrizos and the sort of welcome he could expect next time he turned up on Privet Drive.
　　On their last evening, Mrs. Lopez conjured up a sumptuous dinner that included all of Roberto's favorite things, ending with a mouthwatering treacle pudding. Pablo and Luis rounded off the evening with a display of Filibuster fireworks; they fiIled the kitchen with red and blue stars that bounced from ceiling to wall for at least half an hour. Then it was time for a last mug of hot chocolate and bed.
　　It took a long while to get started next morning. They were up at dawn, but somehow they still seemed to have a great deal to do.
　　65
　　Mrs. Lopez dashed about in a bad mood looking for spare socks and quills; people kept colliding on the stairs, half-dressed with bits of toast in their hands; and Mr. Lopez nearly broke his neck, tripping over a stray chicken as he crossed the yard carrying Martina's trunk to the car.
　　Roberto couldn't see how eight people, six large trunks, two owls, and a rat were going to fit into one small Ford Anglia. He had reckoned, of course, without the special features that Mr. Lopez had added.
　　"Not a word to Alicia," he whispered to Roberto as he opened the. trunk and showed him how it had been magically expanded so that the luggage fitted easily.
　　When at last they were all in the car, Mrs. Lopez glanced into the back seat, where Roberto, Diego, Pablo, Luis, and Miguel were all sitting comfortably side by side, and said, "Muggles do know more than we give them credit for, don't they?" She and Martina got into the front seat, which had been stretched so that it resembled a park bench. "I mean, you'd never know it was this roomy from the outside, would you?"
　　Mr. Lopez started up the engine and they trundled out of the yard, Roberto turning back for a last look at the house. He barely had time to wonder when he'd see it again when they were back Luis had forgotten his box of Filibuster fireworks. Five minutes after that, they skidded to a halt in the yard so that Pablo could run in for his broomstick. They had almost reached the highway when Martina shrieked that she'd left her diary. By the time she had clambered back into the car, they were running very late, and tempers were running high.

　　Mr. Lopez glanced at his watch and then at his wife.

　　Roberto had caught the Hogwarts Express the previous year. The tricky part was getting onto platform nine and three-quarters, which wasn't visible to the Muggle eye. What you had to do was walk through the solid barrier dividing platforms nine and ten. It didn't hurt, but it had to be done carefully so that none of the Muggles noticed you vanishing.
　　"Miguel first," said Mrs. Lopez, looking nervously at the clock overhead, which showed they had only five minutes to disappear casually through the barrier.

　　61
　　perfectly confident; this wasn't nearly as uncomfortable as using Floo powder. Both of them bent low over the handles of their trolleys and walked purposefully toward the barrier, gathering speed. A few feet away from it, they broke into a run and
　　CRASH.

Arms reached through the portrait hole to pull Roberto and Diego inside, leaving Carolina to scramble in after then-t.
　　"Brilliant!" yelled Lee Jordan. "Inspired! What an entrance! Flying a car right into the Whomping Willow, people'll be talking about that one for years -"
　　"Good for you," said a fifth year Roberto had never spoken to; someone was patting him on the back as though he'd just won a marathon; Pablo and Luis pushed their way to the front of the crowd and said together, "Why couldn't we've come in the car, eh?"
　　84
　　Diego was scarlet in the face, grinning embarrassedly, but Roberto could see one person who didn't look happy at all. Miguel was visible over the heads of some excited first years, and he seemed to be trying to get near enough to start telling them off. Roberto nudged Diego in the ribs and nodded in Miguel's direction. Diego got the point at once.
　　"Got to get upstairs - bit tired," he said, and the two of them started pushing their way toward the door on the other side of the room, which led to a spiral staircase and the dormitories.
　　"'Night," Roberto called back to Carolina, who was wearing a scowl just like Miguel's.
　　They managed to get to the other side of the common room, still having their backs slapped, and gained the peace of the staircase. They hurried up it, right to the top, and at last reached the door of their old dormitory, which now had a sign on it saying SECOND YEARS. They entered the familiar, circular room, with its five four-posters hung with red velvet and its high, narrow windows. Their trunks had been brought up for them and stood at the ends of their beds.

　　CHAPTER Six
　　GILDEROY LOCKHART
　　he next day, however, Roberto barely grinned once. Things started to go downhill from breakfast in the Great Hall. The four long house tables were laden with tureens of porridge, plates of kippers, mountains of toast, and dishes of eggs and bacon, beneath the enchanted ceiling (today, a dull, cloudy gray). Roberto and Diego sat down at the Gryffindor table next to Carolina, who had her copy of Voyages with Vampires propped open against a milk jug. There was a slight stiffness in the way she said "Morning," which told Roberto that she was still disapproving of the way they had arrived. Mauricio Silva, on the other hand, greeted them cheerfully. Mauricio was a round-faced and accident-prone boy with the worst memory of anyone Roberto had ever met.

　　streamed in, circling the hall and dropping letters and packages into the chattering crowd. A big, lumpy package bounced off Mauricio's head and, a second later, something large and gray fell into Carolina's jug, spraying them all with milk and feathers.

　　"STEALING THE CAR, I WOULDN'T HAVE BEEN SURPRISED IF THEY'D EXPELLED YOU, YOU WAIT TILL I GET HOLD OF YOU, I DON'T SUPPOSE YOU STOPPED TO THINK WHAT YOUR FATHERAND I WENT THROUGH WHEN WE SAW IT WAS GONE -"

　　"Well, I don't know what you expected, Diego, but you -"

　　"Exactly," said Wood. He was a tall and burly sixth year and, at the moment, his eyes were gleaming with a crazed enthusiasm. "It's part of our new training program. Come on, grab your broom, and let's go," said Wood heartily. "None of the other teams have started training yet; we're going to be first off the mark this year -"

　　on each team who carry clubs to beat the Bludgers away from their side. Pablo and Luis Lopez are the Gryffindor Beaters."

　　122
　　October arrived, spreading a damp chill over the grounds and into the castle. Madam Pomfrey, the nurse, was kept busy by a sudden spate of colds among the staff and students. Her Pepperup potion worked instantly, though it left the drinker smoking at the ears for several hours afterward. Martina Lopez, who had been looking pale, was bullied into taking some by Miguel. The steam pouring from under her vivid hair gave the impression that her whole head was on fire.
　　Raindrops the size of bullets thundered on the castle windows for days on end; the lake rose, the flower beds turned into muddy streams, and Villalba's pumpkins swelled to the size of garden sheds. Oliver Wood's enthusiasm for regular training sessions, however, was not dampened, which was why Roberto was to be found, late one stormy Saturday afternoon a few days before Halloween, returning to Gryffindor Tower, drenched to the skin and splattered with mud..
　　123
　　Even aside from the rain and wind it hadn't been a happy practice session.
　　Pablo and Luis, who had been spying on the Slytherin team, had seen for themselves the speed of those new Nimbus Two Thousand and Ones. They reported that the Slytherin team was no more than seven greenish blurs, shooting through the air like missiles.
　　As Roberto squelched along the deserted corridor he came across somebody who looked just as preoccupied as he was. Nearly Headless Nick, the ghost of Gryffindor Tower, was staring morosely out of a window, muttering under his breath, ". . . don't fulfill their requirements . . . half an inch, if that . . ."
　　"Hello, Nick," said Roberto.
　　"Hello, hello," said Nearly Headless Nick, starting and looking round. He wore a dashing, plumed hat on his long curly hair, and a tunic with a ruff, which concealed the fact that his neck was almost completely severed. He was pale as smoke, and Roberto could see right through him to the dark sky and torrential rain outside.
　　"You look troubled, young Gonzales," said Nick, folding a transparent letter as he spoke and tucking it inside his doublet.
　　"So do you," said Roberto.
　　"Ah," Nearly Headless Nick waved an elegant hand, "a matter of no importance. . . . It's not as though I really wanted to join. . . . Thought I'd apply, but apparently I 'don't fulfill requirements' -"
　　In spite of his airy tone, there was a look of great bitterness on his face.
　　"But you would think, wouldn't you," he erupted suddenly, pulling the letter back out of his pocket, "that getting hit forty-five times in the neck with a blunt axe would qualify you to join the Headless Hunt?"
　　124
　　"Oh - yes," said Roberto, who was obviously supposed to agree.
　　"I mean, nobody wishes more than I do that it had all been quick and clean, and my head had come off properly, I mean, it would have saved me a great deal of pain and ridicule. However -" Nearly Headless Nick shook his letter open and read furiously: "'We can only accept huntsmen whose heads have parted company with their bodies. You will appreciate that it would be impossible otherwise for members to participate in hunt activities such as Horseback Head-Juggling and Head Polo. It is with the greatest regret, therefore, that I must inform you that you do not fulfill our requirements.
　　With very best wishes, Sir Patrick Delaney-Podmore.'"
　　Fuming, Nearly Headless Nick stuffed the letter away.
　　"Half an inch of skin and sinew holding my neck on, Roberto! Most people would think that's good and beheaded, but oh, no, it's not enough for Sir Properly Decapitated-Podmore."
　　Nearly Headless Nick took several deep breaths and then said, in a far calmer tone, "So - what's bothering you? Anything I can do?"
　　"No," said Roberto. "Not unless you know where we can get seven free Nimbus Two Thousand and Ones for our match against Sly -"
　　The rest of Roberto's sentence was drowned out by a high-pitched mewling from somewhere near his ankles. He looked down and found himself gazing into a pair of lamp-like yellow eyes. It was Mrs. Norris, the skeletal gray cat who was used by the caretaker, Argus Filch, as a sort of deputy in his endless battle against students.
　　"You'd better get out of here, Roberto," said Nick quickly. "Filch isn't in a good mood - he's got the flu and some third years accidentally plastered frog brains all over the ceiling in dungeon five. He's been cleaning all morning, and if he sees you dripping mud all over the place -"
　　.125
　　"Right," said Roberto, backing away from the accusing stare of Mrs. Norris, but not quickly enough. Drawn to the spot by the mysterious power that seemed to connect him with his foul cat, Argus Filch burst suddenly through a tapestry to Roberto's right, wheezing and looking wildly about for the rule-breaker.
　　There was a thick tartan scarf bound around his head, and his nose was unusually purple.
　　"Filth!" he shouted, his jowls aquiver, his eyes popping alarmingly as he pointed at the muddy puddle that had dripped from Roberto's Quidditch robes.
　　"Mess and muck everywhere! I've had enough of it, I tell you! Follow me, Gonzales!"
　　So Roberto waved a gloomy good-bye to Nearly Headless Nick and followed Filch back downstairs, doubling the number of muddy footprints on the floor.
　　Roberto had never been inside Filch's office before; it was a place most students avoided. The room was dingy and windowless, lit by a single oil lamp dangling from the low ceiling. A faint smell of fried fish lingered about the place. Wooden filing cabinets stood around the walls; from their labels, Roberto could see that they contained details of every pupil Filch had ever punished. Pablo and Luis Lopez had an entire drawer to themselves. A highly polished collection of chains and manacles hung on the wall behind Filch's desk. It was common knowledge that he was always begging Dominguez to let him suspend students by their ankles from the ceiling.
　　Filch grabbed a quill from a pot on his desk and began shuffling around looking for parchment.
　　"Dung," he muttered furiously, "great sizzling dragon bogies . . . frog brains . . . rat intestines . . . I've had enough of it . . . make an example . . . where's the form . . . yes . . ."
　　.126
　　He retrieved a large roll of parchment from his desk drawer and stretched it out in front of him, dipping his long black quill into the ink pot.
　　"Name . . . Roberto Gonzales. Crime . . ."
　　"It was only a bit of mud!" said Roberto.
　　"It's only a bit of mud to you, boy, but to me it's an extra hour scrubbing!"
　　shouted Filch, a drip shivering unpleasantly at the end of his bulbous nose.
　　"Crime . . . befouling the castle . . . suggested sentence . . ."
　　Dabbing at his streaming nose, Filch squinted unpleasantly at Roberto who waited with bated breath for his sentence to fall.
　　But as Filch lowered his quill, there was a great BANG! on the ceiling of the office, which made the oil lamp rattle.
　　"PEEVES!" Filch roared, flinging down his quill in a transport of rage. "I'll have you this time, I'll have you!"
　　And without a backward glance at Roberto, Filch ran flat-footed from the office, Mrs. Norris streaking alongside him.
　　Peeves was the school poltergeist, a grinning, airborne menace who lived to cause havoc and distress. Roberto didn't much like Peeves, but couldn't help feeling grateful for his timing. Hopefully, whatever Peeves had done (and it sounded as though he'd wrecked something very big this time) would distract Filch from Roberto.
　　Thinking that he should probably wait for Filch to come back, Roberto sank into a moth-eaten chair next to the desk. There was only one thing on it apart from his half-completed form: a large, glossy, purple envelope with silver lettering on the front. With a quick glance at the door to check that Filch wasn't on his way back, Roberto picked up the envelope and read: kwikspell A Correspondence Course in Beginners' Magic.
　　.127
　　Intrigued, Roberto flicked the envelope open and pulled out the sheaf of parchment inside. More curly silver writing on the front page said: Feel out of step in the world of modern magic? Find yourself making excuses not to perform simple spells? Ever been taunted for your woeful wandwork? There is an answer! Kwikspell is an all-new, fail-safe, quick-result, easy-learn course. Hundreds of witches and wizards have benefited from the Kwikspell method! Madam Z. Nettles of Topsham writes: "I had no memory for incantations and my potions were a family joke! Now, after a Kwikspell course, I am the center of attention at parties and friends beg for the recipe of my Scintillation Solution!" Warlock D. J. Prod of Didsbury says: "My wife used to sneer at my feeble charms, but one month into your fabulous Kwikspell course and I succeeded in turning her into a yak! Thank you, Kwikspell!"
　　 Fascinated, Roberto thumbed through the rest of the envelope's contents. Why on earth did Filch want a Kwikspell course? Did this mean he wasn't a proper wizard? Roberto was just reading "Lesson One: Holding Your Wand (Some Useful Tips)" when shuffling footsteps outside told him Filch was coming back. Stuffing the parchment back into the envelope, Roberto threw it back onto the desk just as the door opened.
　　Filch was looking triumphant.
　　"That vanishing cabinet was extremely valuable!" he was saying gleefully to Mrs. Norris. "We'll have Peeves out this time, my sweet -"
　　His eyes fell on Roberto and then darted to the Kwikspell envelope, which, Roberto realized too late, was lying two feet away from where it had started.
　　Filch's pasty face went brick red. Roberto braced himself for a tidal wave of fury. Filch hobbled across to his desk, snatched up the envelope, and threw it into a drawer.
　　"Have you - did you read -?" he sputtered.
　　.128
　　"No," Roberto lied quickly.
　　Filch's knobbly hands were twisting together.
　　"If I thought you'd read my private - not that it's mine - for a friend - be that as it may - however -"
　　Roberto was staring at him, alarmed; Filch had never looked madder. His eyes were popping, a tic was going in one of his pouchy cheeks, and the tartan scarf didn't help.
　　"Very well - go - and don't breathe a word - not that - however, if you didn't read - go now, I have to write up Peeves' report - go -"
　　Amazed at his luck, Roberto sped out of the office, up the corridor, and back upstairs. To escape from Filch's office without punishment was probably some kind of school record.
　　"Roberto! Roberto! Did it work?"
　　Nearly Headless Nick came gliding out of a classroom. Behind him, Roberto could see the wreckage of a large black-and-gold cabinet that appeared to have been dropped from a great height.
　　"I persuaded Peeves to crash it right over Filch's office," said Nick eagerly.
　　"Thought it might distract him -"
　　"Was that you?" said Roberto gratefully. "Yeah, it worked, I didn't even get detention. Thanks, Nick!"
　　They set off up the corridor together. Nearly Headless Nick, Roberto noticed, was still holding Sir Patrick's rejection letter..
　　.129
　　"I wish there was something I could do for you about the Headless Hunt,"
　　Roberto said.
　　Nearly Headless Nick stopped in his tracks and Roberto walked right through him. He wished he hadn't; it was like stepping through an icy shower.
　　"But there is something you could do for me," said Nick excitedly. "Roberto - would I be asking too much - but no, you wouldn't want -"
　　"What is it?" said Roberto.
　　"Well, this Halloween will be my five hundredth deathday," said Nearly Headless Nick, drawing himself up and looking dignified.
　　"Oh," said Roberto, not sure whether he should look sorry or happy about this.
　　"Right."
　　"I'm holding a party down in one of the roomier dungeons. Friends will be coming from all over the country. It would be such an honor if you would attend. Mr. Lopez and Miss Fernandez would be most welcome, too, of course - but I daresay you'd rather go to the school feast?" He watched Roberto on tenterhooks.
　　"No," said Roberto quickly, "I'll come -"
　　"My dear boy! Roberto Gonzales, at my deathday party! And" - he hesitated, looking excited - "do you think you could possibly mention to Sir Patrick how very frightening and impressive you find me?"
　　"Of - of course," said Roberto.
　　Nearly Headless Nick beamed at him. "A deathday party?" said Carolina keenly when Roberto had changed at last and joined her and Diego in the common room. "I bet there aren't many living people who can say they've been to one of those - it'll be fascinating!".
　　.130
　　"Why would anyone want to celebrate the day they died?" said Diego, who was halfway through his Potions homework and grumpy. "Sounds dead depressing to me. . . ."
　　Rain was still lashing the windows, which were now inky black, but inside all looked bright and cheerful. The firelight glowed over the countless squashy armchairs where people sat reading, talking, doing homework or, in the case of Pablo and Luis Lopez, trying to find out what would happen if you fed a Filibuster firework to a salamander. Pablo had "rescued" the brilliant orange, fire-dwelling lizard from a Care of Magical Creatures class and it was now smouldering gently on a table surrounded by a knot of curious people.
　　Roberto was at the point of telling Diego and Carolina about Filch and the Kwikspell course when the salamander suddenly whizzed into the air, emitting loud sparks and bangs as it whirled wildly round the room. The sight of Miguel bellowing himself hoarse at Pablo and Luis, the spectacular display of tangerine stars showering from the salamander's mouth, and its escape into the fire, with accompanying explosions, drove both Filch and the Kwikspell envelope from Roberto's mind. By the time Halloween arrived, Roberto was regretting his rash promise to go to the deathday party. The rest of the school was happily anticipating their Halloween feast; the Great Hall had been decorated with the usual live bats, Villalba's vast pumpkins had been carved into lanterns large enough for three men to sit in, and there were rumors that Dominguez had booked a troupe of dancing skeletons for the entertainment.
　　"A promise is a promise," Carolina reminded Roberto bossily. "You said you'd go to the deathday party."
　　So at seven o'clock, Roberto, Diego, and Carolina walked straight past the doorway to the packed Great Hall, which was glittering invitingly with gold plates and candles, and directed their steps instead toward the dungeons.
　　.131
　　The passageway leading to Nearly Headless Nick's party had been lined with candles, too, though the effect was far from cheerful: These were long, thin, jet-black tapers, all burning bright blue, casting a dim, ghostly light even over their own living faces. The temperature dropped with every step they took. As Roberto shivered and drew his robes tightly around him, he heard what sounded like a thousand fingernails scraping an enormous blackboard.
　　"Is that supposed to be music?" Diego whispered. They turned a corner and saw Nearly Headless Nick standing at a doorway hung with black velvet drapes.
　　"My dear friends," he said mournfully. "Welcome, welcome . . . so pleased you could come. . . ."
　　He swept off his plumed hat and bowed them inside.
　　It was an incredible sight. The dungeon was full of hundreds of pearly-white, translucent people, mostly drifting around a crowded dance floor, waltzing to the dreadful, quavering sound of thirty musical saws, played by an orchestra on a raised, black-draped platform. A chandelier overhead blazed midnight-blue with a thousand more black candles. Their breath rose in a mist before them; it was like stepping into a freezer.
　　"Shall we have a look around?" Roberto suggested, wanting to warm up his feet.
　　"Careful not to walk through anyone," said Diego nervously, and they set off around the edge of the dance floor. They passed a group of gloomy nuns, a ragged man wearing chains, and the Fat Friar, a cheerful Hufflepuff ghost, who was talking to a knight with an arrow sticking out of his forehead. Roberto wasn't surprised to see that the Bloody Baron, a gaunt, staring Slytherin ghost covered in silver bloodstains, was being given a wide berth by the other ghosts.
　　.132
　　"Oh, no," said Carolina, stopping abruptly. "Turn back, turn back, I don't want to talk to Moaning Myrtle -"
　　"Who?" said Roberto as they backtracked quickly.
　　"She haunts one of the toilets in the girls' bathroom on the first floor," said Carolina.
　　"She haunts a toilet?"
　　"Yes. It's been out-of-order all year because she keeps having tantrums and flooding the place. I never went in there anyway if I could avoid it; it's awful trying to have a pee with her wailing at you -"

　　On the other side of the dungeon was a long table, also covered in black velvet. They approached it eagerly but next moment had stopped in their tracks, horrified. The smell was quite disgusting. Large, rotten fish were laid on handsome silver platters; cakes, burned charcoal-black, were heaped on salvers; there was a great maggoty haggis, a slab of cheese covered in furry green mold and, in pride of place, an enormous gray cake in the shape of a tombstone, with tar-like icing forming the words, Sir Nicholas de Mimsy-Porpington died 31st October, 1492 Roberto watched, amazed, as a portly ghost approached the table, crouched low, and walked through it, his mouth held wide so that it passed through one of the stinking salmon.
　　"Can you taste it if you walk though it?" Roberto asked him.
　　"Almost," said the ghost sadly, and he drifted away.
　　.133
　　"I expect they've let it rot to give it a stronger flavor," said Carolina knowledgeably, pinching her nose and leaning closer to look at the putrid haggis.
　　"Can we move? I feel sick," said Diego.
　　They had barely turned around, however, when a little man swooped suddenly from under the table and came to a halt in midair before them.
　　"Hello, Peeves," said Roberto cautiously.
　　Unlike the ghosts around them, Peeves the Poltergeist was the very reverse of pale and transparent. He was wearing a bright orange party hat, a revolving bow tie, and a broad grin on his wide, wicked face.
　　"Nibbles?" he said sweetly, offering them a bowl of peanuts covered in fungus.
　　"No thanks," said Carolina.

　　The squat ghost of a girl had glided over. She had the glummest face Roberto had ever seen, half-hidden behind lank hair and thick, pearly spectacles.
　　"What?" she said sulkily.

　　.134
　　Myrtle sniffed.
　　"Miss Fernandez was just talking about you -" said Peeves slyly in Myrtle's ear.

　　"Oh, yeah -"
　　"She did -"

　　"You've forgotten pimply," Peeves hissed in her ear.
　　Moaning Myrtle burst into anguished sobs and fled from the dungeon.

　　Pimply!"

　　Nearly Headless Nick now drifted toward them through the crowd.
　　.135
　　"Enjoying yourselves?"
　　"Oh, yes," they lied.

　　The horses galloped into the middle of the dance floor and halted, rearing and plunging. At the front of the pack was a large ghost who held his bearded head under his arm, from which position he was blowing the horn.
　　The ghost leapt down, lifted his head high in the air so he could see over the crowd (everyone laughed), and strode over to Nearly Headless Nick, squashing his head back onto his neck.

　　.136
　　"Very amusing," said Nearly Headless Nick darkly.

　　Roberto was very cold by now, not to mention hungry.

　　"Pudding might not be finished yet," said Diego hopefully, leading the way toward the steps to the entrance hall.
　　.137
　　And then Roberto heard it.
　　". . . rip . . . tear . . . kill . . ."
　　It was the same voice, the same cold, murderous voice he had heard in Lockhart's office.

　　". . . kill . . . time to kill . . ."

　　"This way," he shouted, and he began to run, up the stairs, into the entrance hall. It was no good hoping to hear anything here, the babble of talk from the Halloween feast was echoing out of the Great Hall. Roberto sprinted up the marble staircase to the first floor, Diego and Carolina clattering behind him.
　　"Roberto, what're we -"
　　"SHH!"
　　.138
　　Roberto strained his ears. Distantly, from the floor above, and growing fainter still, he heard the voice: ". . . I smell blood. . . . I SMELL BLOOD!"

　　the caretaker's cat, was hanging by her tail from the torch bracket. She was stiff as a board, her eyes wide and staring.

　　But it was too late. A rumble, as though of distant thunder, told them that the feast had just ended. From either end of the corridor where they stood came the sound of hundreds of feet climbing the stairs, and the loud, happy talk of well-fed people; next moment, students were crashing into the passage from both ends.
　　The chatter, the bustle, the noise died suddenly as the people in front spotted the hanging cat. Roberto, Diego, and Carolina stood alone, in the middle of the corridor, as silence fell among the mass of students pressing forward to see the grisly sight.
　　Then someone shouted through the quiet.
　　"Enemies of the Heir, beware! You'll be next, Mudbloods!"
　　It was Javier Sosa. He had pushed to the front of the crowd, his cold eyes alive, his usually bloodless face flushed, as he grinned at the sight of the hanging, immobile cat.
　　CHAPTER NINE
　　THE WRTITING ON THE WALL
　　What's going on here? What's going on?" Attracted no doubt by Sosa's shout, Argus Filch came shouldering his way through the crowd. Then he saw Mrs. Norris and fell back, clutching his face in horror.
　　"My cat! My cat! What's happened to Mrs. Norris?" he shrieked.
　　And his popping eyes fell on Roberto.

　　"Argus!"
　　Dominguez had arrived on the scene, followed by a number of other teachers. In seconds, he had swept past Roberto, Diego, and Carolina and detached Mrs. Norris from the torch bracket.
　　"Come with me, Argus," he said to Filch. "You, too, Mr. Gonzales, Mr. Lopez, Miss Fernandez."
　　Lockhart stepped forward eagerly.
　　*140*
　　"My office is nearest, Headmaster - just upstairs - please feel free -"
　　"Thank you, Gilderoy," said Dominguez.
　　The silent crowd parted to let them pass. Lockhart, looking excited and important, hurried after Dominguez; so did Professors Romero and Peralta.
　　As they entered Lockhart's darkened office there was a flurry of movement across the walls; Roberto saw several of the Lockharts in the pictures dodging out of sight, their hair in rollers. The real Lockhart lit the candles on his desk and stood back. Dominguez lay Mrs. Norris on the polished surface and began to examine her. Roberto, Diego, and Carolina exchanged tense looks and sank into chairs outside the pool of candlelight, watching.
　　The tip of Dominguez's long, crooked nose was barely an inch from Mrs. Norris's fur. He was looking at her closely through his half-moon spectacles, his long fingers gently prodding and poking. Professor Romero was bent almost as close, her eyes narrowed. Peralta loomed behind them, half in shadow, wearing a most peculiar expression: It was as though he was trying hard not to smile. And Lockhart was hovering around all of them, making suggestions.
　　"It was definitely a curse that killed her - probably the Transmogrifian Torture - I've seen it used many times, so unlucky I wasn't there, I know the very countercurse that would have saved her . .....
　　Lockhart's comments were punctuated by Filch's dry, racking sobs. He was slumped in a chair by the desk, unable to look at Mrs. Norris, his face in his hands. Much as he detested Filch, Roberto
　　*141*
　　couldn't help feeling a bit sorry for him, though not nearly as sorry as he felt for himself If Dominguez believed Filch, he would be expelled for sure.
　　Dominguez was now muttering strange words under his breath and tapping Mrs. Norris with his wand but nothing happened: She continued to look as though she had been recently stuffed.
　　". . . I remember something very similar happening in Ouagadogou," said Lockhart, "a series of attacks, the full story's in my autobiography, I was able to provide the townsfolk with various amulets, which cleared the matter up at once ......
　　The photographs of Lockhart on the walls were all nodding in agreement as he talked. One of them had forgotten to remove his hair net.
　　At last Dominguez straightened up.
　　"She's not dead, Argus," he said softly.
　　Lockhart stopped abruptly in the middle of counting the number of murders he had prevented.
　　"Not dead?" choked Filch, looking through his fingers at Mrs. Norris. "But why's she all - all stiff and frozen?"
　　"She has been Petrified," said Dominguez ("Ah! I thought so!" said Lockhart). "But how, I cannot say . . . ."
　　"Ask him!" shrieked Filch, turning his blotched and tearstained face to Roberto.
　　"No second year could have done this," said Dominguez firmly. "it would take Dark Magic of the most advanced -"
　　"He did it, he did it!" Filch spat, his pouchy face purpling. "You saw what he wrote on the wall! He found - in my office - he knows I'm a - I'm a -" Filch's face worked horribly. "He knows I'm a Squib!" he finished.
　　142
　　"I never touched Mrs. Norris!" Roberto said loudly, uncomfortably aware of everyone looking at him, including all the Lockharts on the walls. "And I don't even know what a Squib is."
　　"Rubbish!" snarled Filch. "He saw my Kwikspell letter!"
　　"If I might speak, Headmaster," said Peralta from the shadows, and Roberto's sense of forboding increased; he was sure nothing Peralta had to say was going to do him any good.
　　"Gonzales and his friends may have simply been in the wrong place at the wrong time," he said, a slight sneer curling his mouth as though he doubted it. "But we do have a set of suspicious circumstances here. Why was he in the upstairs corridor at all? Why wasn't he at the Halloween feast?"
　　Roberto, Diego and Carolina all launched into an explanation about the deathday party. ". . . there were hundreds of ghosts, theyll tell you we were there -"
　　"But why not join the feast afterward?" said Peralta, his black eyes glittering in the candlelight. "Why go up to that corridor?"
　　Diego and Carolina looked at Roberto.
　　"Because - because -" Roberto said, his heart thumping very fast; something told him it would sound very far-fetched if he told them he had been led there by a bodiless voice no one but he could hear, "because we were tired and wanted to go to bed," he said.
　　"Without any supper?" said Peralta, a triumphant smile flickering across his gaunt face. "I didn't think ghosts provided food fit for living people at their parties."
　　"We weren't hungry," said Diego loudly as his stomach gave a huge rumble.
　　Peralta's nasty smile widened.
　　*143*
　　"I suggest, Headmaster, that Gonzales is not being entirely truthful," he said. "It might be a good idea if he were deprived of certain privileges until he is ready to tell us the whole story. I personally feel he should be taken off the Gryffindor Quidditch team until he is ready to be honest."
　　"Really, Carlos," said Professor Romero sharply, "I see no reason to stop the boy playing Quidditch. This cat wasn't hit over the head with a broomstick. There is no evidence at all that Gonzales has done anything wrong."
　　Dominguez was giving Roberto a searching look. His twinkling light- blue gaze made Roberto feel as though he were being X-rayed.
　　"Innocent until proven guilty, Carlos," he said firmly.
　　Peralta looked furious. So did Filch.
　　"My cat has been Petrified!" he shrieked, his eyes popping. "I want to see some punishment!"
　　"We will be able to cure her, Argus," said Dominguez patiently. "Professer Sprout recently managed to procure some Mandrakes. As soon as they have reached their full size, I will have a potion made that will revive Mrs. Norris."
　　"I'll make it," Lockhart butted in. "I must have done it a hundred times. I could whip up a Mandrake Restorative Draught in my sleep -"
　　"Excuse me," said Peralta icily. "But I believe I am the Potions master at this school."
　　There was a very awkward pause.

Ancient and shriveled, many people said he hadn't noticed he was dead. He had simply got up to teach one day and left his body behind him in an armchair in front of the staff room fire; his routine had not varied in the slightest since.

　　CHAPTER TEN
　　THE ROGUE BLUDGER
　　ince the disastrous episode of the pixies, Professor Lockhart had not brought live creatures to class. Instead, he read passages from his books to them, and sometimes reenacted some of the more dramatic bits. He usually picked Roberto to help him with these reconstructions; so far, Roberto had been forced to play a simple Transylvanian villager whom Lockhart had cured of a Babbling Curse, a yeti with a head cold, and a vampire who had been unable to eat anything except lettuce since Lockhart had dealt with him.
　　Roberto was hauled to the front of the class during their very next Defense Against the Dark Arts lesson, this time acting a werewolf If he hadn't had a very good reason for keeping Lockhart in a good mood, he would have refused to do it.
　　"Nice loud howl, Roberto - exactly - and then, if you'll believe it, I pounced - like this - slammed him to the floor - thus with one hand, I managed to hold him down - with my other, I
　　*161*
　　put my wand to his throat -I then screwed up my remaining strength and performed the immensely complex Homorphus Charm - he let out a piteous moan - go on, Roberto - higher than that - good - the fur vanished - the fangs shrank - and he turned back into a man. Simple, yet effective - and another village will remember me forever as the hero who delivered them from the monthly terror of werewolf attacks."
　　The bell rang and Lockhart got to his feet.
　　"Homework - compose a poem about my defeat of the Wagga Wagga Werewolf! Signed copies of Magical Me to the author of the best one!"
　　The class began to leave. Roberto returned to the back of the room, where Diego and Carolina were waiting.
　　"Ready?" Roberto muttered.

"But the thing is, it's in the Restricted Section of the library, so I need a teacher to sign for it - I'm sure it would help me understand what you say in Gadding with Ghouls about slow-acting venoms
　　"Ah, Gadding with Ghouls!" said Lockhart, taking the note from Carolina and smiling widely at her. "Possibly my very favorite book. You enjoyed it?"
　　-162
　　"Oh, yes," said Carolina eagerly. "So clever, the way you trapped that last one with the tea-strainer -"

　　Five minutes later, they were barricaded in Moaning Myrtle's out-of- order bathroom once again. 　　Roberto woke early on Saturday morning and lay for a while thinking about the coming Quidditch match. He had never wanted to beat Slytherin so badly. 　　CHAPTER ELEVEN
　　THE D-KJEL]ING C-L-IJIB
　　Roberto woke up on Sunday morning to find the dormitory blazing with winter sunlight and his arm reboned but very stiff. He sat up quickly and looked over at Colin's bed, but it had been blocked from view by the high curtains Roberto had changed behind yesterday. Seeing that he was awake, Madam Pomfrey came bustling over with a breakfast tray and then began bending and stretching his arm and fingers.
　　"All in order," she said as he clumsily fed himself porridge lefthanded. "When you've finished eating, you may leave."
　　Roberto dressed as quickly as he could and hurried off to Gryffindor Tower, desperate to tell Diego and Carolina about Colin and Dobby, but they weren't there. Roberto left to look for them, wondering where they could have got to and feeling slightly hurt that they weren't interested in whether he had his bones back or not.
　　*182*

　　"We'd've come to meet you, but we decided to get started on the Polyjuice Potion," Diego explained as Roberto, with difficulty, locked the stall again. "We've decided this is the safest place to hide it."

　　Professor Flitwick this morning. That's why we decided we'd better get going -"

　　"There's something else," said Roberto, watching Carolina tearing bundles of knotgrass and throwing them into the potion. "Dobby came to visit me in the middle of the night."
　　Diego and Carolina looked up, amazed. Roberto told them everything Dobby had told him - or hadn't told him. Carolina and Diego listened with their mouths open.

　　*184*
　　The news that Colin Creevey had been attacked and was now lying as though dead in the hospital wing had spread through the entire school by Monday morning. The air was suddenly thick with rumor and suspicion. The first years were now moving around the castle in tight-knit groups, as though scared they would be attacked if they ventured forth alone.
　　Martina Lopez, who sat next to Colin Creevey in Charms, was distraught, but Roberto felt that Pablo and Luis were going the wrong way about cheering her up. They were taking turns covering themselves with fur or boils and jumping out at her from behind statues. They only stopped when Miguel, apoplectic with rage, told them he was going to write to Mrs. Lopez and tell her Martina was having nightmares.
　　Meanwhile, hidden from the teachers, a roaring trade in talismans, amulets, and other protective devices was sweeping the school. Mauricio Silva bought a large, evil-smelling green onion, a pointed purple crystal, and a rotting newt tail before the other Gryffindor boys pointed out that he was in no danger; he was a pure- blood, and therefore unlikely to be attacked.
　　"They went for Filch first," Mauricio said, his round face fearful. "And everyone knows I'm almost a Squib."
　　In the second week of December Professor Romero came around as usual, collecting names of those who would be staying at school for Christmas. Roberto, Diego, and Carolina signed her list; they had heard that Sosa was staying, which struck them as very suspicious. The holidays would be the perfect time to use the Polyjuice Potion and try to worm a confession out of him.
　　Unfortunately, the potion was only half finished. They still
　　* 3-85*
　　needed the bicorn horn and the boomslang skin, and the only place they were going to get them was from Peralta's private stores. Roberto privately felt he'd rather face Slytherin's legendary monster than let Peralta catch him robbing his office.

　　potion. When Peralta turned and walked off to bully Mauricio, Carolina caught Roberto's eye and nodded.
